## 7.0 Derivation Of Mean X And Variance S2 Of Bearing Error When Random Variable Is Rectangularly Distributed 7.1 Derivation Of Mean Bearing Error X

General expressions for the mean and variance of bearing error shall be derived that will apply to all the temperature categories CA, B, C, D, E, and F) into which different VOR receivers are classified. 

An example using category C shall be derived. 

The mean value, X, of bearing error due to temperature variation is defined as: 
n 

l:  X·  ~Yi  ],  (30)  X =  i=1  h  Z  t.Yi  i=1 
where t.Yi represents the ith interval of the random variable (temperature). 

~~i::-is equal to the -total temperature range (dependent upon the category.) 
Xi represents the average temperature over the interval of length t.Yi . 

If the increments of temperature t.Yi are assumed to be uniform for all i, equation (30) modifies to 

n  t.Y  Z  X·  ],  (31 )  X =  i=1  Temperature Range in Degrees C 
For purposes of laboratory measurement of bearing error due to temperature variation, Xi for all i is defined: 

$${\rm X_{\hat{1}}}=\frac{{\rm X_{\hat{1}}}+{\rm X_{\hat{1}+\Delta Y}}}{2}\tag{32}$$
where Xy denotes the bearing error measured at temperature Y, Xy + fj.Y denotes the bearing error measured at temperature Y + fj.Y. 

Equation (31) now modifies to 

$$\overline{X}=\frac{\Delta Y}{n}\ \left\{\left[\ \frac{X_{Y}+X_{Y}+\Delta Y}{2}\ \right]\ +\right.\tag{33}$$
Equation (33) now is a general expression for X 
applicable to all categories with Y corresponding to the lowest value of temperature. 

Note that equation (33) does not have weighting factors because the temperature distribution is rectangular. 

When the temperature range is an integral multiple of ~Y, K = 1. 

When the temperature range is not an integral multiple of ~Y. K > 1. 

As an example, let equation (33) be applied to category C whose temperature range is -40 to +55 degrees C. 

Letting ~Y = 10 degrees C and Y = 40 degrees C equation (33) becomes 

X  + X  X  +X  X  +X  X+X  -10  0 + 0  +10  -30  +  -30  -20  . -40 2  2  +  X  +X  -20  -10 +  2  [ 2  2  (34)  x  +  +10  +X  X  +X  . X  +X.  X  +X  {X  +X  +10/2) 1  +2.0 +  +20 - *c+30* +  +30  +40 +  ~ '3-40  +50~+- - +50  +5.-0-·  .  .1  2  2  2  2  2  2 
Equation (34) simplifies to 

+ 2~50 + 1~55 ]  4  4  (35) 
This is identical to equation (8). 

Equation (35) utilizes the laboratory data directly (with the standard conditiog error X
R 
subtracted out) to determine the mean bearing error X for Category C. 

The mean bearing errors of the other categories are derived as follows: 

(1) 
Choose the proper value of temperature range. 
(2) 
Let Y equal the lowest value of temperature in the range. 
(3) 
Let ~Y = 10 degrees C. 
(4) 
Choose the proper value of K.

# !Ff~N~!~ £ Receiver Rf Input Voltage (Hard And Easy Microvolts)

--`,``,````,`,`,``,``,````,`````-`-`,,`,,`,`,,`---

## Receiver Rf Signal Levels

Modern RF signal generators are usually calibrated in dBm (decibels with respect to 1 milliwatt into 50 ohms), and in volts, millivolts, or microvolts into a 50 ohm termination. 

(Note that a dBm = .224 volts.) 
Therefore, this unit of measurement will be used throughout this document. 

Older RF signal generators were calibrated in open circuit voltage, or emf, and would deliver one-half of the open-circuit voltage into a terminating impedance equal to the generator source impedance. 

Historically, sensitivity standards for VOR, localizer and glide slope receivers were originally set up using emf-calibrated generators. 

When the termination-relat~d signal generators came into use, it was not considered desirable to change the sensitivity values. 

Since the termination-related generator delivered twice as much voltage into the receiver as an emf-rated generator, it was easier to meet sensitivity standards, and the termination-rated voltages were known as "easy" 
microvol ts. 

In order to get back to the harder-to-meet levels ("hard" 
microvolts). a 6 dB pad was connected between the termination-rated generator and the receiver. 

As a result of the almost universal availability of 50 ohm measurement use and synthesis equipment, the dBm as a unit of measurement has been adopted in this document. 

Therefore, when using this document with a 50 ohm generator, a 6 dB 
attenuator is not necessary and the generator may be read directly.
